    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="39920c0dc9af48b6aab4dd898fcf8508">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'eb0f15d2-b4b1-470b-b679-faeea2f42490'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

Martinez</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d187354176" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) is the most promising anode for the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries (NIBs); however, a general mechanism for sodium storage in HC remains unclear, obstructing the development of highly efficient anodes for NIBs. To elucidate the mechanism of sodium storage in the pores, operando synchrotron small-angle X-ray scattering, wide-angle X-ray scattering, X-ray absorption near edge structure, Raman spectroscopy, and galvanostatic measurements are combined. The multimodal approach provides mechanistic insights into the sodium pore-filling process for different HC microstructures including the pore sizes that are preferentially filled, the extent to which different pore sizes are filled, and how the defect concentration influences pore filling. It is observed that sodium in the larger pores has an increased pseudo-metallic sodium character consistent with larger sodium clusters. Furthermore, it is shown that the HCs prepared at higher pyrolysis temperatures have a larger capacity from sodium stored in the pores and that sodium intercalation between graphene layers occurs simultaneously with the pore filling in the plateau region. Opportunities are outlined to improve the performance of HC anodes by fully utilizing the pores for sodium storage, helping to pave the way for the commercialization of sodium ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302171-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Currently, batteries are believed to be the best technology for the grid-scale storage of energy derived from intermittent renewable energy sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0001_R_d187353982e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) offer a solution to concerns about the sustainability, safety, cost, and supply of lithium resources for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0001_R_d187353982e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0004_R_d187353982e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Using abundant, ethical, and environmentally benign sodium resources, NIBs are emerging as a viable candidate for grid-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0002_R_d187353982e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0005_R_d187353982e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While cathodes for NIBs with equivalent performance to LIBs have been reported, the anode material is a major bottleneck in the full-cell performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0004_R_d187353982e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0006_R_d187353982e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0007_R_d187353982e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Due to sodium's larger ionic radius and higher ionization potential compared to lithium, the graphite-based anodes used in LIBs are not suitable for NIBs as sodium ions cannot efficiently intercalate between the graphene layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0008_R_d187353982e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0009_R_d187353982e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon (HC), a disordered carbonaceous material with randomly oriented graphitic layers, is a promising anode for NIBs due to its high capacity, electrochemical stability, and cost-effectiveness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0010_R_d187353982e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">10</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0013_R_d187353982e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Large graphite interlayer spacing values (0.36–0.40&nbsp;nm),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0014_R_d187353982e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Proc. R. Soc. Lond.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> curved graphitic domains, and nanopores make HC a suitable anode for achieving high reversible capacity and cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>HC consists of large granular particles and each particle is nanoporous. The nanopores within HC particles can be classified as open or closed pores, where open pores are connected with the outside environment and closed pores are isolated within the material, inaccessible to species such as conventional fluids.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0015_R_d187353982e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ChemSusChem">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0017_R_d187353982e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While researchers have used gas adsorption to measure the porosity of the HCs, this technique does not detect closed nanopores. To detect closed pores, other techniques such as small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) are necessary.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0008_R_d187353982e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0017_R_d187353982e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0020_R_d187353982e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ions such as sodium can diffuse through the HC and fill the closed pores; these filled pores provide sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The annealing temperature and precursors used during HC synthesis determine its structure and porosity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, there is agreement that the nanopore size increases with pyrolysis temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0022_R_d187353982e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0027_R_d187353982e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, there is a consensus that the spacing between the turbostratic graphite layers decreases with pyrolysis temperature, however, it does not reach the interlayer spacing of graphite. It has been shown that defect concentrations in the HC matrix decrease with increasing pyrolysis temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0023_R_d187353982e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0028_R_d187353982e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ChemElectroChem">28</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0031_R_d187353982e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>A typical capacity-potential curve of HC presents two electrochemical features: a sloping region at high potentials (≈0.1–2.5&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and a plateau at low potentials (≈0–0.1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). The conditions used to synthesize HC determine the total capacity and the ratio between slope and plateau capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0031_R_d187353982e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are discrepancies in reported sodium storage mechanisms in HC, primarily due to the diversity in HC microstructure, the absence of crystallinity, and the ambiguity between interlayer spacing and nanopores as these can be in similar size regimes. It is widely accepted that during cycling, sodium ions can be stored in HC through a) surface and defect adsorption, including vacancies, heteroatoms, and edges; b) intercalation between graphene layers; and c) filling of nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0015_R_d187353982e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ChemSusChem">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0032_R_d187353982e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Carbon">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the relationship between the capacity regions and the proposed storage mechanisms remains the subject of debate.</p>
         
         <p>There is disagreement on the storage mechanism that occurs during the slope region. Some studies attribute this region to sodium adsorption in defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0022_R_d187353982e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0033_R_d187353982e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0037_R_d187353982e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, Boomier et al. reported a correlation between the slope capacity and the concentration of defects in the graphene sheets, determined from the intensity ratio <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> from the D and G band Raman peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0036_R_d187353982e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, using in situ Raman and density functional theory (DFT), Anji et al. proposed sodium adsorption on surfaces and defect sites during the high voltage slope region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0037_R_d187353982e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, other studies propose sodium intercalation between graphene layers during the slope region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0024_R_d187353982e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0038_R_d187353982e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0039_R_d187353982e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Komaba et al. observed an increase in the (002) d-spacing using ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) when HC was sodiated to 0.1&nbsp;V, indicating an expansion of the interlayer spacing due to Na intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0038_R_d187353982e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Using operando Raman, Weaving et al. reported a decrease in G-peak energy during the slope region due to sodium ions intercalating in the turbostatic layers of HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0039_R_d187353982e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, other studies show that the intercalation between graphene layers occurs during the plateau region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0019_R_d187353982e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0034_R_d187353982e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - CS Nano">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0035_R_d187353982e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0040_R_d187353982e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0041_R_d187353982e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Qiu et&nbsp;al. and Alvin et al. using in situ and ex situ XRD, respectively, observed a constant interlayer d-spacing during the slope process and an increase in interlayer spacing during the plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0040_R_d187353982e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0041_R_d187353982e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These contradictory results can perhaps be attributed to the diversity of HC microstructures, the difficulty in studying amorphous materials, and the similar size regimes of nanopores and interlayer spacings.</p>
         
         <p>In general, there is agreement that the nanopores are filled during the low-voltage plateau region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0022_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0023_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0025_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0027_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0040_R_d187353982e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Using in situ SAXS, Stevens and Dahn observed a decrease in the electron density contrast between the carbon matrix and the pores during this plateau, indicating that the pores are filled during the plateau region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0024_R_d187353982e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Morikawa et al. also observed the filling of the pores during the plateau region using ex situ SAXS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More recently, using X-ray pair distribution function (XPDF) and <sup>23</sup>Na NMR, Joshua et al. showed that the sodium filling the nanopores during the plateau becomes increasingly metallic and results in the formation of metallic clusters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0025_R_d187353982e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The work summarized above demonstrates the great progress in understanding the relationships between the HC structure and how the sodium is stored in these materials. It is generally agreed that the pores fill during the plateau region, however, questions remain on the nature of the HC porosity, how the pores are filled with sodium (uniformly or non-uniformly), and how the pore filling depends on both potential and HC microstructure. Understanding the porosity and its contribution to the storage of sodium ions will facilitate the design of HC microstructures with improved capacity and reversibility.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we explore how the pyrolysis temperature affects the HC microstructure, including the pore size distribution and volume fraction of closed pores, and we correlate these microstructure features to the resulting electrochemistry. By combining operando SAXS, ex situ X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES), and electrochemical studies, we reveal new insights into sodium storage in pores including the pore sizes that are preferentially filled, the extent to which these are filled, and HC factors influencing pore filling. Our study provides valuable insights into engineering HC nanoporosity to achieve better Na-ion battery performance. Broadly, we envision that our quantitative methodology will open new paths toward studying the storage of ions in porous materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302171-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302171-sec-0030-title">2.1 Microstructure Correlated to Electrochemistry</h3>
            
            <p>Three pristine HC structures prepared at different pyrolysis temperatures were studied to determine the porosity, the defect concentration, and the interlayer spacing/ordering, using SAXS, Raman, and wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS), respectively. Pyrolysis temperatures of 1100, 1400, and 2000&nbsp;°C were investigated, denoted here as HC-1100, HC-1400, and HC-2000&nbsp;°C, respectively. To compare different types of HC, we define “state of sodiation” (SoS) where 0% SoS is pristine HC and 100% is the capacity at C/20 with a cutoff voltage of 0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>Each HC has a different potential–capacity curve. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1a</a>, HCs prepared at lower temperatures present a higher slope capacity fraction, a smaller plateau capacity fraction, and a higher plateau potential compared to HCs prepared at higher temperatures. The contribution from the slope and the plateau are quantified in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The materials are ranked in terms of their performance, with HC-1400&nbsp;°C having the highest performance, followed by HC-2000 and HC-1100&nbsp;°C showing the lowest performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3b94a8ff-a1f0-427b-a819-31ddc18834d1/aenm202302171-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3b94a8ff-a1f0-427b-a819-31ddc18834d1/aenm202302171-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fdf0ccc9-b720-40ba-b8cd-c3af6f05b0b9/aenm202302171-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3b94a8ff-a1f0-427b-a819-31ddc18834d1/aenm202302171-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Normalized capacity-potential curves of the different HC structures. b) SAXS profiles for pristine HCs. The horizontal axis is the scattering vector (<i>Q</i>), defined as <i>Q</i> = 4πsinθ/λ where <i>θ</i> is half the scattering angle and <i>λ</i> is the X-ray wavelength. The vertical axis is the absolute intensity of the scattered X-rays (in&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>). c) Example of a SAXS fit for HC-1400&nbsp;°C showing the contribution from HC particles at low <i>Q</i>, nanopores, and a constant background. d) Pore size distribution obtained from modeling the nanopore region of the SAXS data for each pristine HC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">SAXS was performed to measure the pore size distribution and volume fraction of pores for the three pristine HC structures. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the SAXS profiles of each pristine HC. Each HC presents a different SAXS curve, showing a different microstructure; HCs prepared at higher temperatures have higher scattering intensity from the nanopores and the shoulder is at lower <i>Q</i> values indicating more and larger pores. Three main contributions to the scattered intensity can be observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1c</a>); i) a power law at low <i>Q</i> that arises from scattering from the large granular HC particles (<i>I</i><sub>particles</sub>), ii) a shoulder region ≈0.1–0.4&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> due to scattering from nanopores (<i>I</i><sub>pores</sub>) and a background term (<i>I</i><sub>bckg</sub>) that comes from the tail of interatomic scattering (peak at ≈1.7&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>). Thus, the overall intensity can be modeled by the combination of these three terms:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202302171-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2b06ab34-2253-4418-adb3-7807fdb42db8/aenm202302171-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202302171:aenm202302171-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202302171-math-0001.png">

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202302171-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi>C</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\ {I}_{{\mathrm{bckg}}} = \ C\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>A</i> is proportional to the total surface area from the large particles, <i>k</i> is the slope from Porod's law that accounts for the roughness of the particle interfaces, where <i>k</i> = 4 for a perfectly smooth interface, and <i>C</i> is a constant.</div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To determine the pore size distribution, we modeled the <i>I</i><sub>pores</sub>(<i>Q</i>). We assume spherical pores (see Supporting Information) and a dilute system; thus, the scattering intensity from highly polydisperse nanopores can be modeled with a Schulz-Zimm size distribution such that

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202302171-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                                    <mn>1</mn>

                                       <mi>z</mi>

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\sigma \ = {R}_{{\mathrm{avg}}}\ \sqrt {\frac{1}{{z + 1}}} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The Schulz–Zimm distribution was chosen as it captures the data well and has been implemented previously for porous media.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0042_R_d187353982e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The use of a dilute limit (non-interacting pores) is valid since the porosity is not too large (vide infra) and there is a large polydispersity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0043_R_d187353982e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The pristine HC SAXS curves were fit with this model and the pore size distribution was determined (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1d</a>) along with a mean pore size and the RMSD, the latter is directly proportional to the polydispersity. Also, it should be noted that the Porod's exponent <i>k</i> at low <i>Q</i> from the HC particles slightly increases with temperature, with <i>k =</i> 3.80 for HC-1100&nbsp;°C, <i>k =</i> 3.84 for HC-1400&nbsp;°C, and <i>k =</i> 3.90 for HC-2000&nbsp;°C, possibly indicating that at higher pyrolysis temperatures the interface between large particles is smoother.</p>
            
            <p>While determining the closed porosity is challenging using pycnometry, because helium gas does not reach the closed pores, SAXS can be used as it is sensitive to the closed pores. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0002">2</a> shows a schematic view of a HC cross-section. To determine the volume fraction of closed pores <i>∅</i> (<i>V</i><sub>closed pores</sub>/<i>V</i><sub>particle</sub>) and the electron density of the HC matrix using SAXS—in absolute intensities and normalized to the container thickness—we used the measured packing density of the HC particles, the measured density of HC particles which includes the closed pores (using helium displacement), the calculated volume fraction of particles <i>∅</i>* (<i>V</i><sub>particles</sub>/<i>V</i><sub>container</sub>), and the scattering invariant (<i>Q</i>*, see Supporting Information). Here, we assume that the electron density in the pores is negligible. These calculations are shown in Supporting Information and the results are summarized in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202302171-tbl-0001">1</a>.</p>

               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c32c96fe-ff89-436a-97aa-cf2fa9ff7de8/aenm202302171-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Based on the SAXS model for the pristine HCs, the HC materials synthesized at higher temperatures have larger mean pore radius – ranging from 5.1&nbsp;Å radius for HC-1100&nbsp;°C to 9.2&nbsp;Å radius for HC-2000&nbsp;°C – and have a higher polydispersity and higher volume fraction of closed pores. These microstructural properties are correlated with a smaller slope capacity fraction, larger plateau capacity fraction, and lower plateau potential (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1a</a>). The observed increase in mean pore radius with increasing pyrolysis temperature is consistent with the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e1388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e1388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The relative defect concentration and graphitic order in the HC materials were determined by Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). Raman spectra of the three HCs show the typical D band ≈1350&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and the G band ≈1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are correlated with defective sp<sup>2</sup> structures (<i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> breathing mode vibrations) and ordered sp<sup>2</sup> carbons as in graphite (<i>E</i><sub>2g</sub> breathing mode vibrations), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0044_R_d187353982e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Phys. Rev. B.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To get additional insight, we deconvoluted the Raman spectra into four energy bands: S, D, A, and G bands, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0030_R_d187353982e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Carbon">30</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0045_R_d187353982e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Carbon">45</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0047_R_d187353982e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S-band located ≈1100<i>–</i>1200&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to rings containing 7+ carbons, and the A band between the D and G is assigned to vacancy point defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0047_R_d187353982e1432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the D and A bands relate to defects in the material, whereas the G band represents the graphitic order. Thus, the ratio between the band areas, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>A</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, is used to estimate the defect concentration. As quantitatively summarized in Supporting Information, both ratios decrease with pyrolysis temperature, indicating that higher pyrolysis temperature reduces defects, such as vacancies, which is in accordance with the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0023_R_d187353982e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0028_R_d187353982e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ChemElectroChem">28</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0031_R_d187353982e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The full-width at half maximum (FWHM) intensity of the D band also decreases with pyrolysis temperature, another indicator that the carbon matrix becomes less defective.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0048_R_d187353982e1456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Faraday Discuss.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We can see that the HCs with higher defect concentrations (lower pyrolysis temperatures) are correlated with a longer slope capacity, which in the literature has been attributed to sodium adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0033_R_d187353982e1461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0037_R_d187353982e1461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>WAXS was used to further study the local graphitic ordering of the pristine HCs. The WAXS profiles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information) show two broad peaks ≈1.7 and 3&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> (<i>d</i>≈3.7&nbsp;Å and ≈2&nbsp;Å), corresponding, respectively, to the (002) plane spacing between graphene layers and the (100) plane spacing from in-plane atomic order in the graphene sheets. As the pyrolysis temperature increases, the (002) peak shifts to higher <i>Q</i> values, and the full-width at half maximum (FWHM) decreases; these changes correspond to a decrease in the interlayer spacing and an increase in local order, respectively. The interlayer spacing is quantitatively summarized in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202302171-tbl-0001">1</a> and is consistent with values reported previously. Note a sharp graphite (002) reflection in the HC-2000&nbsp;°C sample that shows a fraction of the material is crystalline graphite. We estimate this represents 3% of the HC-2000&nbsp;°C. From the (002) peak and the (100) peak FWHMs, we can obtain the coherence length of the local order of the layer stacking (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) and within the graphene plane (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>), respectively, by using the Scherrer equation (see Supporting Information for details).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0049_R_d187353982e1487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Mathematisch-Physikalische Klasse">49</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0052_R_d187353982e1487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Energy Fuels">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#aenm202302171-tbl-0001">1</a>, both <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> increase with pyrolysis temperature corresponding to larger graphitic domains. Furthermore, from <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>, we obtain the average number of locally ordered stacked graphene layers, varying from ≈3 graphene layers for HC-1100&nbsp;°C to ≈8 layers for HC-2000&nbsp;°C.</p>
            
            <p>Following our systematic analysis of the microstructure dependence with HC pyrolysis temperature, we conclude that by increasing the pyrolysis temperature there is an increase in the local ordering of the graphitic layers, specifically the interlayer spacing decreases and the graphitic domains increase in size. This is consistent with other observations in the literature for other HC materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0023_R_d187353982e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0031_R_d187353982e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We hypothesize that the increased order in graphitic domains leads to larger pore size distributions; this in turn gives rise to a higher closed pore volume fraction available for the storage of sodium ions.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302171-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Pores Are Filled During the Plateau Region</h3>
            
            <p>We performed operando SAXS to understand the relationship between HC microstructure and how the pores are filled during cycling, including how the pore size distribution changes with SoS and which pores are preferentially filled with sodium ions depending on the microstructure.</p>
            
            <p>The SAXS scattering intensities from nanopores are proportional to the average of the square of the electron density difference between the carbon matrix and the pores. Therefore, when sodium ions fill the closed pores, the scattering intensity will decrease. Our operando SAXS is only sensitive to the closed pores as the open pores are filled with electrolyte, which has a similar electron density to the carbon matrix, making the open pores indistinguishable from the carbon matrix. During the slope region of HC-1400&nbsp;°C, the scattering intensity from the nanopores slightly increases, indicating that the nanopores do not fill during this region and that the electrochemically inserted Na ions cause an increase in the average electron density of the carbon matrix, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0003">3a</a>. During the plateau region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0003">3b</a>) the scattering intensity from nanopores decreases markedly and the SAXS curve shape changes, which shows that the pores fill with sodium and the overall pore size distribution changes. Furthermore, during the plateau, we see that the scattering intensity from nanopores decreases non-uniformly with respect to <i>Q</i>, demonstrating that the pores fill non-uniformly (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6–S8</a>, Supporting Information). In this work, we use the term non-uniform to indicate that not all pores are being filled simultaneously. Similar trends can be seen for HC-1100 and HC-2000&nbsp;°C as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/01ff97ea-8ac2-4dc6-9e19-a19ab26d9c1b/aenm202302171-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/01ff97ea-8ac2-4dc6-9e19-a19ab26d9c1b/aenm202302171-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15970c52-f00b-48df-8156-6789e26b513d/aenm202302171-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/01ff97ea-8ac2-4dc6-9e19-a19ab26d9c1b/aenm202302171-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">operando SAXS profiles for the first sodiation of HC-1400&nbsp;°C during a) the slope capacity region and b) the plateau capacity region. c) Examples of SAXS data (solid lines) and fits (dashed lines) of HC-1400&nbsp;°C at various stages of sodiation, vertically offset for clarity. d) Potential curve of HC-1400&nbsp;°C with the stages of sodiation marked with color circles for c).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">Because different physical processes occur during the slope and plateau regions, shown by the different qualitative changes in the SAXS data, we used two different models to fit these data. The SAXS model used to fit the slope region (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202302171-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>) allows for the determination of the mean pore radius, pore size polydispersity, and the electron density contrast, and accounts for changes in the pore size distribution and the HC matrix electron density due to the electrochemical insertion of Na ions. To model SAXS in the plateau region, we used a distribution of the electron density within the pores to account for non-uniform pore filling. This modification allows us to determine how the pore filling depends on pore size and whether the pore filling is uniform or nonuniform. Therefore, the SAXS intensity from the nanopores in the plateau is given by:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202302171-disp-0009"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
