                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(9)</span></div>where <i>ρ</i><sub>scale</sub> is a pore electron density scaled to a maximum value of 0.278 e<sup>−</sup>&nbsp;Å<sup>−3</sup> which is the theoretical electron density from metallic sodium that would result if the pores are fully filled with Na (see Supporting Information), <i>erf(z)</i> is the error function, <i>R</i><sub>i</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ii</sub> are the lower and upper pore radius limits of the error function, which can be thought of as the minimum and maximum pore radius that are filled with Na ions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), and <i>σ</i><sub>r</sub> is the standard deviation of the error function, which was set equal to 1&nbsp;Å. This function was chosen as it can flexibly describe non-uniform pore filling. The fitting parameters in this model include the mean pore radius, polydispersity, pore electron density scale, <i>R</i><sub>i</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ii</sub>. The pore size distribution was allowed to vary to account for expansion and shrinkage as pores accommodate sodium ions. Using this model, we can distinguish how the pores fill with sodium ions and the pore size distribution of the overall, filled, and empty pores.</div>
            
            <p>SAXS profiles for HC-1400&nbsp;°C with their corresponding fits at different stages of sodiation are given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0003">3c</a>, including two profiles in the slope region and three in the plateau region. The fits for HC-1100 and HC-2000&nbsp;°C can be found in Supporting Information. The SAXS models used for the slope and plateau regions accurately fit the data of HC-1400 and HC-2000&nbsp;°C. We attribute the decreased quality of the fit for HC-1100&nbsp;°C to the lower scattering from nanopores (fewer nanopores resulting in lower SAXS intensity) and thus the more dominant background.</p>
            
            <p>Before implementing the distribution model outlined above, we tested a variety of models. The qualitative pore-filling trend from all these different models was similar. Simple models—such as one population of pores where all the pores were filled uniformly—were discarded as they did not capture the non-uniform filling of the pores shown by the <i>Q</i>-dependent decrease in the SAXS (see Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6–S8</a>, Supporting Information), exemplified by larger chi-squared values for HC-2000&nbsp;°C (see Supporting Information). More complex models—such as three populations of filled pores, where intermediate pore sizes were filled with sodium whereas larger and smaller sizes remained empty—were considered, however, the distribution model—where we applied a distribution to the electron density of the pores—was chosen as it describes the non-uniform filling and can determine the different extents that the pores fill, making this a more flexible model. Comparison between these different models can be found in Supporting Information.</p>
            
            <p>In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0004">4</a>, the magnitude of the average electron density contrast undergoes small changes during the slope region for all HCs, but decreases markedly during the plateau region, which is consistent with the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0018_R_d187353982e1746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0024_R_d187353982e1746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the slope region, the magnitude of the average electron density contrast was obtained as a fitting parameter from Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202302171-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>. In the plateau region, this contrast was calculated by subtracting the average electron density of the carbon matrix from the calculated average electron density of the pores. The average matrix electron density was assumed constant along the plateau region and was set equal to that obtained at the end of the slope. This calculation is shown in Supporting Information. We attribute the small density increase during the slope region to Na ions adsorbing in defect sites from both the carbon matrix and the pore walls. HC-1100&nbsp;°C shows more scattered variation in the magnitude of the average electron density contrast during the slope region that is due to weaker SAXS. The decrease in the average electron density contrast during the plateau region is due to Na ions filling the pores. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), upon desodiation the opposite processes occur; there is an increase in the average electron density during the plateau region and this remains practically constant during the slope region, indicating that the filling of the pores is qualitatively a reversible process. The electron density of fully desodiated HC-2000&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) is almost the same as the pristine HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0004">4</a>), indicating the reversibility of sodium ions stored in the pores. Conversely, for HC-1100&nbsp;°C, the electron density in the fully desodiated state is lower than the pristine state, suggesting lower reversibility, consistent with the electrochemistry (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c04c28a8-c43f-4001-a6c4-d372f86d3b8e/aenm202302171-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c04c28a8-c43f-4001-a6c4-d372f86d3b8e/aenm202302171-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7262bad1-25a7-4105-9fba-21b2ede43139/aenm202302171-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c04c28a8-c43f-4001-a6c4-d372f86d3b8e/aenm202302171-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Magnitude of the average difference in electron density contrast (dotted line) between the carbon matrix and the pores upon sodiation with their corresponding potential curves (solid line) for a) HC-1100, b) HC-1400, and c) HC-2000&nbsp;°C. The gray shadow distinguishes the sloping region from the plateau in the potential curves. The small increase in density for HC-2000&nbsp;°C in the slope may be due to less Na adsorption.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302171-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium Storage Mechanism in Closed Pores</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0005">5</a> shows how the pores are filled during the plateau region, where the continuous lines represent the HC pore size distribution and the filled dashed curves show the extent to which the pores are filled with sodium. A detailed example of these distributions can be found in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). For HC-1100&nbsp;°C, the smaller pores, up to ≈4&nbsp;Å in radius, fill initially until they are fully filled. Upon further sodiation, larger pores start to fill up to ≈6&nbsp;Å in radius. For HC-1400&nbsp;°C, intermediate-sized pores, between ≈6 and ≈8&nbsp;Å in radius, start to fill first and upon further sodiation, smaller pores between ≈3 and ≈6&nbsp;Å in radius fill until these pores are fully filled with sodium. For HC-2000&nbsp;°C, we observe two processes. Mid-sized pores, between ≈8 and ≈10&nbsp;Å in radius, fill until ≈50% SoS, and then sodium begins to fill the larger and smaller pores, reaching pores between ≈4 and ≈18&nbsp;Å in radius. Our model cannot distinguish between the complete filling of some pores and the incomplete filling of all pores. Generally, we observe that at lower pyrolysis temperatures, smaller pores preferentially fill and are fully filled at 100% SoS, meanwhile in HCs synthesized at higher temperatures, average pore sizes preferentially fill, with a wider range of pore sizes incompletely filled at 100% SoS. During desodiation, we observed the opposite trend, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/877c455e-3ab2-406f-aef5-f0efdee067ab/aenm202302171-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/877c455e-3ab2-406f-aef5-f0efdee067ab/aenm202302171-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2868b988-9b28-47ce-abe0-1cbe7636b957/aenm202302171-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/877c455e-3ab2-406f-aef5-f0efdee067ab/aenm202302171-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Normalized HC pore size distributions (continuous line) of all HCs at different SoS along the plateau capacity of the first sodiation. Dashed curves and filled regions represent the pore distribution that is filled with sodium ions for each state of charge. The distribution curves at different stages of sodiation are vertically offset for clarity. The distributions have been normalized (Equation S5, Supporting Information) such that when the electron density inside the pore is equal to that of metallic sodium (0.278&nbsp;e<sup>−</sup>&nbsp;Å<sup>−3</sup>), it represents a fully filled pore.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Ex situ XANES at the Na K-edge was performed to investigate the sodium oxidation state during sodiation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6a</a>, we see slight changes in the spectra upon sodiation, a qualitative indicator that the local environment of sodium changes. The edge position was obtained from the first maximum of the XANES first derivative curve. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6b</a>, a trend is observed where the edge position for all HCs shifts to lower energies upon sodiation, indicating an average reduction of the sodium. In HCs prepared at higher pyrolysis temperatures, the sodium is more reduced at 100% SoS suggesting a more pseudo-metallic character. We hypothesize that larger sodium clusters are formed, with an increased metallic character, in the larger pores. Meanwhile for HC-1100&nbsp;°C, the change in the edge position is smaller, suggesting that Na maintains its ionic nature at 100% SoS. The lower temperature HC has more defects, and thus we propose that the small pores are filled mainly by sodium adsorbing in defective sites in the pore wall, therefore sodium largely maintains its ionic character. Interestingly, the shape of the potential-capacity curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0001">1a</a>) presents similar trends to the relative sodium oxidation states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6b</a>). Spectra for HC-1100 and HC-1400&nbsp;°C are given in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The decreased quality in the spectra for HC-1100 and HC-1400&nbsp;°C is due to a combination of lower resolution and decreased count time.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ff73b4e3-b360-4022-9673-fcfd2e14d0ba/aenm202302171-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ff73b4e3-b360-4022-9673-fcfd2e14d0ba/aenm202302171-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0a6a760-4d28-42ed-ace8-a5431755ef27/aenm202302171-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ff73b4e3-b360-4022-9673-fcfd2e14d0ba/aenm202302171-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Total fluorescence yield (TFY) Na K-edge XANES spectra of HC-2000&nbsp;°C at various stages of sodiation with Na metal and NaCl references. b) Edge positions relative to sodium metal for the three HC types at different stages during sodiation. The dashed gray and black lines represent the edge positions of sodium metal and NaCl, respectively. For HC-1100 and the HC-1400&nbsp;°C and HC-2000&nbsp;°C at lower SoS, the Na K-edge position is at higher energies than for NaCl which may be due to Na in HC being surrounded by more electronegative elements than Cl, such as oxygen (≈1–10&nbsp;wt.% oxygen has been reported in HC),<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0023" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0023_R_d187353982e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0034" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0034_R_d187353982e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - CS Nano">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0035" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0035_R_d187353982e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or due to different sodium coordination environment compared to crystalline NaCl.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The mechanism that we deduce from our operando SAXS and ex situ XANES builds on previous DFT calculations,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0054_R_d187353982e1884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">54</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0057_R_d187353982e1884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which propose that the sodium storage in pores occurs in two sequential steps: i) sodium adsorption to a defect site in the pore wall, and ii) growth of a sodium cluster within the pore and consequent pore filling. The first process occurs during the slope region. As DFT studies suggest that the adsorption of sodium on a perfect graphene layer is unfavorable,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0053_R_d187353982e1889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Chem. A">53</a>-<a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0056_R_d187353982e1889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - npj Comput. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> a sodium ion must be first adsorbed in a defect site at the pore wall. As shown by Yong et al., the formation energy of a sodium atom adsorbing in monovacancies and divacancies in graphene sheets is ≈−1&nbsp;eV relative to Na/Na<sup>+1</sup>, showing that this process is one of the origins of the slope capacity (0.1–2&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0056_R_d187353982e1899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - npj Comput. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The second process of pore filling predominantly occurs during the plateau region. After a sodium ion is adsorbed in a defect site of the pore wall during the slope region, a small cluster is formed, possibly Na<sub>4</sub>, as DFT calculations suggest this is more energetically stable than a single sodium atom in a defect site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0056_R_d187353982e1906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - npj Comput. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, sodium clusters grow by the attachment of additional sodium ions to fill the pore. Thus, the nanopore filling is linked to the defect concentration in the pore walls.</p>
            
            <p>To develop an explanation for the different nanopore filling for each HC microstructure, we combined the proposed nanopore filling mechanism from operando SAXS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0005">5</a>) with measurements of the HC defect concentration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information), and the reduction of sodium upon HC sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6</a>). We assume that the total HC defect concentration obtained by Raman is proportional to the defect concentration in the pore walls. Thus, the HC synthesized at higher temperatures has a lower concentration of defects in the pore walls. DFT calculations based on slit-shaped pores suggest that it is more energetically favorable to fill smaller pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0056_R_d187353982e1924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - npj Comput. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We propose a mechanism consistent with this for HC-1100&nbsp;°C with the smaller pores initially filling as this is more thermodynamically favorable. The filling of these pores is dominated by sodium adsorption due to the high defect concentration, and thus, the sodium in the pores for HC-1100&nbsp;°C is mainly ionic, consistent with XANES (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6b</a>). Once the smaller pores are fully filled, the larger pores begin to fill; however, the growth of these clusters might be limited due to the high defect concentration, favoring adsorption on the pore walls, and leading to the formation of more small clusters that fill the pore rather than a single large cluster. For the higher temperature HC-1400 and HC-2000&nbsp;°C, where the defect concentration is lower, the average size pores fill first, and have an increased pseudo-metallic sodium character (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0006">6b</a>); this is consistent with the formation of larger clusters. We propose that the pore-filling process for the higher temperatures is dictated by the tradeoff between the disfavorable filling of small pores, due to the lower defect concentration, and the less energetically favored formation of larger clusters in the larger pores. Upon further sodiation, smaller pores fill for HC-1400&nbsp;°C, and both smaller and larger pores fill for HC-2000&nbsp;°C. The defect concentration in pore walls and the number and size of pores play key roles in determining the cluster size and the maximum amount of sodium ions that can be stored in pores, influencing both the plateau and total capacities.</p>
            
            <p>Even though our model cannot distinguish between some fully filled pores or all the pores being partly filled, we speculate that the smallest filled pores from HC-2000&nbsp;°C are fully filled at the end of sodiation. This suggestion is based on the behavior of the lower temperature HC where the smaller pores are fully filled and our hypothesis that it is energetically more favorable to fully fill a partly filled pore and that some pores may not be accessible due to diffusion limitations and/or low defect concentrations. The changes in the mean pore size are due to the electrochemical insertion of sodium in the HC (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0007">7</a>). At cell-scale, expansion/shrinking of the electrodes upon cycling has been observed by researchers using dilatometry, demonstrating the dynamic nature of the HC microstructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0057_R_d187353982e1944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">57</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0058_R_d187353982e1944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dc528d88-30c7-4588-a191-1484648f1709/aenm202302171-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/dc528d88-30c7-4588-a191-1484648f1709/aenm202302171-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b0d8d83a-5e71-4913-b6c7-beb50adba37b/aenm202302171-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dc528d88-30c7-4588-a191-1484648f1709/aenm202302171-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Variation of the mean pore size of the empty, filled, and overall pores as a function of SoS through the plateau region, for a) HC-1100, b) HC-1400, and c) HC-2000&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302171-sec-0060-title">2.4 Larger Pore Size Distributions Provide More Capacity from the Pores</h3>
            
            <p>Focusing on the importance of quantification,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0059_R_d187353982e1976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Environ. Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we quantified the capacity of Na ions stored in pores and compared this with the plateau capacity obtained from electrochemical measurements, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8a</a>. As detailed in Supporting Information, the capacity from sodium in the pores is determined from the measured average electron density within the pores, the porosity of the carbon, and the active mass of the HC electrode. If during the plateau region, the sodium was only stored in the pores, we would obtain a line with a unity slope, as represented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8a</a> with the solid line. We observe for the three HC types that the pores do not account for all the Na storage capacity, showing that there are other Na storage mechanisms during the low voltage plateau region. The larger average pore size, polydispersity, and total volume fraction of pores for the higher temperatures, results in a larger plateau capacity and increased storage of sodium in nanopores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8a</a>). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8b</a>, we see that the percentage of the plateau capacity due to pore filling increases with carbonization temperature.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/698007d6-2321-477b-bf79-8af1cb270fc1/aenm202302171-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/698007d6-2321-477b-bf79-8af1cb270fc1/aenm202302171-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/799f2113-f5cd-44d0-96bf-43c6a0303622/aenm202302171-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/698007d6-2321-477b-bf79-8af1cb270fc1/aenm202302171-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparison of the plateau capacity obtained from the electrochemical measurements with the calculated capacity from sodium in the pores obtained from SAXS. The solid line represents the scenario where the plateau capacity is completely accounted for by pore filling. b) Correlation between pyrolysis temperature and the percentage of the plateau capacity that originates from pore filling. c) Calculated volume fraction of filled pores in the plateau region for the three HC types.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on reports in the literature showing the expansion of the interlayer spacing between graphene layers in the plateau region, we hypothesize that the other contribution to the plateau process comes from sodium intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0019_R_d187353982e2019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0034_R_d187353982e2019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - CS Nano">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0035_R_d187353982e2019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0040_R_d187353982e2019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0041_R_d187353982e2019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we suggest that intercalation and pore filling occur simultaneously in the plateau capacity region, with the relative contribution of these mechanisms depending on the HC microstructure. Because at lower temperatures, the interlayer spacing is larger and there is a lower volume fraction of pores, we propose that during the plateau Na intercalation between graphene layers is favorable. Thus, there is a higher contribution from intercalation, resulting in a lower pore-filling contribution during the plateau as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8</a>. At higher pyrolysis temperatures, the interlayer spacing is smaller and the volume fraction of pores is larger; thus, it is more difficult for sodium ions to intercalate and there is a higher fraction of sodium stored in closed pores.</p>
            
            <p>We propose that the plateau capacity originates from a combination of pore filling and intercalation. Thus, the theoretical plateau capacity depends on the porosity, the pore size distribution, the defect concentration in the pore walls, and the spacing between graphene layers. Furthermore, the plateau voltage is related to the chemical potential of sodium ions intercalating into the HC and sodium ions filling the pores. A qualitative trend can be seen where the HCs with smaller pores filled with smaller sodium clusters have a higher plateau potential. We hypothesize that this is because the average sodium binding energy of small clusters is stronger, with more interaction with the pore wall. This is consistent with the ionic nature of the sodium.</p>
            
            <p>From our multimodal characterization, we illustrate in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0009">9</a> a picture of how the HC microstructure depends on pyrolysis temperature and how this determines the Na pore filling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302171-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee56dd4d-196b-4d71-8c0e-59298460c783/aenm202302171-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee56dd4d-196b-4d71-8c0e-59298460c783/aenm202302171-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b2498127-6a97-48c5-aaeb-ca6ca71ef38d/aenm202302171-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee56dd4d-196b-4d71-8c0e-59298460c783/aenm202302171-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302171-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302171" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic representation of the microstructure and the storage of sodium ions in closed pores for HCs synthesized at different pyrolysis temperatures. The circles represent closed pores whereas the black lines represent graphene layers. The blue scale represents the extent to which the pores are filled with sodium ions; white indicates empty pores while dark blue indicates fully filled pores.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302171-sec-0070-title">2.5 Opportunities to Improve HC Performance</h3>
            
            <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302171-fig-0008">8c</a>, approximately 40% of the pores remain empty after sodiation, providing a significant opportunity to improve the total capacity, and hence performance of HC anodes by fully utilizing the larger pores. As the capacity from the plateau region is more reversible than the slope (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), optimizing sodium storage in pores is critical for the overall performance. We have shown that sodium storage in the pores contributes the most to HC-2000&nbsp;°C, where the closed pores occupy a larger volume fraction and have a larger polydispersity and average pore size. However, regardless of the HC microstructure, the larger pores are less likely to be filled. Finding routes to access these unutilized pores is needed to maximize sodium storage in pores and thus the capacity.</p>
            
            <p>One possible method is to stabilize the formation of larger pseudo-metallic sodium clusters in larger pores, which are less energetically favorable. The addition of defects in the larger pore walls leading to multiple smaller clusters may enable the filling of these larger pores. However, the defect concentration and the pore size cannot be independently controlled with pyrolysis temperature. Furthermore, increasing the defect concentration, which is attributed to the slope region, has negative implications on the reversibility. Thus, new approaches to independently control the HC pore size and the stability of large sodium clusters are required.</p>
            
            <p>Another possible explanation for why some pores are inaccessible to sodium is limited sodium diffusion through HC microstructures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0021_R_d187353982e2078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Our results show that larger pore sizes are more accessible in HC-2000&nbsp;°C. Designing HC materials with improved pathways for the Na-ion diffusion may also facilitate the full utilization of the pores, leading to larger total capacities.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302171-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Combining operando SAXS and electrochemical analysis, we revealed how sodium is stored in the nanopores for different HC microstructures. A relationship was established, where larger plateau capacity regions are attributed to a higher volume fraction of closed pores. From the derived SAXS model, a major finding is that the pores fill non-uniformly, where some pores have a preference to fill. The filling of the pores occurs during the low voltage plateau region, alongside sodium intercalation occurring simultaneously. We have quantified how much the pore filling contributes to the plateau capacity depending on the microstructure. Furthermore, we have shown that not all pores fill, indicating that the pore filling could be increased, leading to longer plateau capacities with higher total capacities for the anode. Our results suggest that the engineering of defect sites in pore walls, together with increasing the pore size distribution and/or volume fraction of closed pores are key to designing superior HC anodes. An improved anode for Na-ion batteries would boost the full-cell performance and help enable commercialization for applications such as grid-scale energy storage, mitigating the sustainability and justice issues of Li-ion batteries. Our study also gives a systematic approach to studying other porous materials and understanding how ions are stored in the pores.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302171-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302171-sec-0120-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Three different HCs were provided by Phillips 66 Company. To prepare the samples, a refinery heavy stream was converted into an oxidized intermediate. HC-1100 and HC-1400&nbsp;°C were further carbonized in a laboratory tube furnace (SentroTech) by holding at the desired set point (1100 and 1400&nbsp;°C, respectively) for 2&nbsp;h with a nitrogen flow at 500 sccm. HC-2000&nbsp;°C was carbonized in a vacuum graphitization furnace (Centorr Vacuum Industries) at 2000&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;min.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302171-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The working electrodes were prepared by mixing 90&nbsp;wt.% of HC, 5&nbsp;wt.% of carbon Super P (Alfa Aesar ≥99%) as a conducting material, and 5&nbsp;wt.% of polyvinylidene (PVDF, MSESupplies ≥99.6%) as a binder in N-Methylpyrrolidone (NMP, MSESupplies ≥99.9%, anhydrous). The resulting slurry was cast on aluminum foil (MTI ≥99.3%) using a 200&nbsp;µm doctor blade. The film was first dried at 100&nbsp;°C using a hot plate for approximately 1 h and then vacuum-dried overnight at 100&nbsp;°C to fully remove NMP. The electrode film was punched into 3.175&nbsp;mm diameter disks for the operando cells, and 14&nbsp;mm diameter disks for the coin cells.</p>
                  
                  <p>The coin cells (CR2032-type) were assembled using the HC electrode as a working electrode and metallic sodium (Alfa Aesar 99.8%) as a counter electrode. The separator used was a glass microfiber filter (Whatman, GF/B, 0.68&nbsp;mm thickness). The electrolyte was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Alfa Aesar ≥99%) in ethylene carbonate (Sigma–Aldrich ≥99%, acid &lt;10&nbsp;ppm, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 10&nbsp;ppm) and dimethyl carbonate (Sigma–Aldrich ≥99%, acid &lt;10&nbsp;ppm, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 10&nbsp;ppm) (1:1 vol%). The cells were galvanostatically charged and discharged in a voltage range of 0–2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na using a potentiostat (Biologic VSP-300) at a current density of 15&nbsp;µA&nbsp;mg<sup>−1</sup>. At the end of the discharge, the voltage was held constant at 0&nbsp;V until the current decayed to 1.5&nbsp;µA&nbsp;mg<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  
                  <p>The operando electrochemical measurements were performed in a modified Swagelok-type cell configuration with a polyether ether ketone (PEEK) body (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The operando cells were prepared in the same way as the coin cells. The cells were assembled inside an Ar-filled glove box, stored in aluminum pouch cells to prevent both O<sub>2</sub> and moisture exposure in case of breakage, and transported to the synchrotron for measurements. The electrochemical measurements were performed under the same conditions. The electrochemistry of the operando cells was compared with the coin cells as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302171-sec-0140-title"> Small-Angle X-Ray Scattering</h4>
                  
                  <p>operando SAXS measurements were performed at beamline 5-ID-D at Advanced Photon Source (APS). Measurements were performed with an X-ray beam energy of 17.5&nbsp;keV (<i>λ</i> = 0.7293&nbsp;Å) using a simultaneous three-detector Rayonix SAXS/MAXS/WAXS system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0060_R_d187353982e2157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Detect. Assoc. Equipment">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the sample geometry, a reduced beam of 0.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;0.25&nbsp;mm was used with an estimated flux of 7e<sup>10</sup> photons&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. Each measurement was performed with 0.3 s exposure time.</p>
                  
                  <p>Six modified Swagelok-type cells were mounted at the beamline using a custom holder and data were simultaneously collected in transmission geometry for each cell every 15 min during charge/discharge (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
                  
                  <p>For the operando experiment, background data were taken using an identical Swagelok cell, but without the HC electrode. Glassy carbon was used to calibrate the intensity for the three detectors. While lanthanum hexaboride, silver behenate, and a silicon diffraction grating were used as a calibration standard to determine the detector geometry and sample-detector distances of 0.2&nbsp;m (WAXS), 1.0&nbsp;m (MAXS), and 8.5&nbsp;m (SAXS). Data were first corrected by transmission, polarization, calibration factor, and exposure time and then reduced using the Nika plugin from the Igor Pro software.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0061_R_d187353982e2175" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The background was subtracted from the data and the 1D corrected SAXS and MAXS were merged using the Irena plugin from the Igor Pro software, obtaining a Q range of 0.008–1&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  
                  <p>Ex situ SAXS measurements of the pristine HCs were also taken in 2&nbsp;mm diameter quartz capillaries. These samples were also corrected for sample thickness to obtain absolute intensities (cm<sup>−1</sup>) and hence the pore volume fraction.</p>
                  
                  <p>The obtained SAXS curves were fitted with a custom Python script using the Levenberg–Marquardt algorithm and the lmfit package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0062_R_d187353982e2191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Zenodo">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302171-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302171-sec-0150-title"> Other Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>WAXS measurements for the pristine HCs were performed at beamline 5-ID-D at APS. Measurements were performed with an X-ray beam energy of 17.5&nbsp;keV in a transmission geometry using 2&nbsp;mm diameter quartz capillaries. Data reduction and background subtraction were performed with the Irena plugin from the Igor Pro software. The WAXS curves were fitted using the lmfit package.</p>
                  
                  <p>Raman spectra were obtained using a Renishaw inVia Raman Microscope with a laser wavelength of 514&nbsp;nm. Samples were scanned for 30 s with a 50x objective lens and using 5% of the laser power. The Raman spectra were fitted using the lmfit package.</p>
                  
                  <p>Ex situ XANES at the Na K-edge for the sodiated HCs was performed at beamline 7.3.1 at Advanced Light Source (ALS) at LBNL. The spectra were recorded in both total fluorescence yield and total electron yield detection modes but only fluorescence yield is shown here. All XANES spectra have been normalized by the incident beam, which is measured by a clean gold mesh. The energy offset was determined using sodium standards and used to calibrate the XANES spectra. HC-1100&nbsp;°C and HC-1400&nbsp;°C XANES spectra were obtained in a different beamtime than HC-2000&nbsp;°C and thus the data quality was different. HC-1100 and HC-1400&nbsp;°C were recorded in the energy range of 1068–1103&nbsp;eV with 1s detection count time and steps of 1&nbsp;eV (pre-edge), 0.2&nbsp;eV (edge), and 0.6&nbsp;eV (post-edge). HC-2000&nbsp;°C was recorded in the energy range of 1050–1236&nbsp;eV with 2s detection count time and steps of 2&nbsp;eV (pre-edge), 0.3&nbsp;eV (edge), and 1&nbsp;eV (post-edge). Coin cells were discharged to desired voltage points, and then, the cells were disassembled inside the Ar-filled glove box and the HC electrodes were rinsed in dimethyl carbonate to remove residual electrolyte and some of the solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0063_R_d187353982e2208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Electrochim. Acta">63</a>, <a href="#aenm202302171-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302171-bib-0064_R_d187353982e2208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Chem. Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrodes were stored in aluminum pouch cells to prevent both O<sub>2</sub> and moisture exposure as the sodiated electrodes are highly reactive. The electrodes were transported to the ALS and measured.</p>
                  
                  <p>True density was measured by helium displacement using a Micromeritics AccuPyc 1330 pycnometer. Prior to the measurement, each sample was dried in a vacuum oven at 110&nbsp;°C overnight to remove moisture. Packing density was measured after tapping the sample (20&nbsp;g) inside a graduated cylinder 1000 times with a Quantachrome Autotap analyzer.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302171-sec-0160-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>The authors gratefully acknowledge Dragan Mejic (CU Boulder) for his assistance during the design and fabrication of the operando X-ray electrochemical cell. X-ray data collection was performed at the DuPont-Northwestern-Dow Collaborative Access Team (DND-CAT) located at Sector 5 of the Advanced Photon Source (APS). DND-CAT was supported by Northwestern University, the Dow Chemical Company, and DuPont de Nemours, Inc. The APS was a U.S. Department of Energy (DOE) Office of Science user facility operated for the DOE Office of Science by Argonne National Laboratory under Contract No. DE-AC02-06CH11357. Raman analyses were performed at MIMIC, CU Boulder (<a target="" class="accessionId" href="https://scicrunch.org/resources/Any/search?q=undefined&amp;l=RRID:SCR_019307" title="Link to external resource: RRID:SCR_019307">RRID:SCR_019307</a>), which was supported, in part, by the U.S. National Science Foundation (Award No. CBET-1338154). This research used resources of the Advanced Light Source, which is a DOE Office of Science User Facility under Contract No. DE-AC02-05CH11231. L.K.I, E.N.A., and M.F.T. acknowledge support from the College of Engineering and Applied Science, the Materials Science Program, and the Department of Chemical and Biological Engineering at the University of Colorado Boulder.</p>

                  <li data-bib-id="aenm202302171-bib-0004"><span class="bullet">4</span><span class="author">J.-Y. Hwang</span>, <span class="author">S.-T. Myung</span>, <span class="author">Y.-K.                      <div class="extra-links getFTR"><span class="hidden data-doi">10.1039/C6CS00776G</span><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div>
                        <span><a
